    II. Introduction
    =20

    2.1 What is VIM?
      =20

    Vim is an improved (in many ways) version of vi, a ubiquitous text= editor=20 found on any UNIX system. VIM was created by Bram=20 Moolenaar with a help of other people. It's free but if you lik= e it=20 you can make a charitable contribution to orphans in Uganda.

    Vim has its own web site, www.v= im.org=20 and several mailing lists<= /a>,=20 with a wealth of information on every aspect of VIM. Vim was succes= sfully=20 ported to nearly all existing OS. It is a default editor in many Li= nux=20 distributions (e.g. RedHat).

    VIM has all features of a modern programmer's editor - macro langu= age,=20 syntax highlighting, customizable user interface, easy integration = with=20 various IDEs plus a set of features which makes VIM so attractive t= o its=20 users: crash recovery, automatic commands, session management.

    VIM has a very broad and loyal user base. Over 10 million people h= ave=20 it installed (counting only Linux users). Estimation is that there = are=20 about half a million people using Vim as their main editor. And thi= s number=20 is growing.

    =20

    2.2 About this Tutorial
      =20

    I started this tutorial for one simple reason - I like regular exp= ressions.=20 Nothing compares to the satisfaction from a well-crafted regexp whi= ch=20 does exactly what you wanted it to do :-). I hope it's passable as = a foreword.=20

    Speaking more seriously, regular expressions (or regexps for short= ) are=20 tools used to manipulate text and data. They don't exist as a stand= alone=20 product but usually are a part of some program/utility. The best kn= own=20 example is UNIX grep, a program to search files for lines th= at=20 match certain pattern. The search pattern is described in terms of = regular=20 expressions. You can think of regexps as a specialized pattern = language.=20 Regexps are quite useful and can greatly reduce time it takes to do= some=20 tedious text editing.

    (Regexp terminology is largely borrowed from Jeffrey Friedl "Maste= ring=20 Regular Expressions.")
    =20

    Many thanks (in no particular order): Benji Fisher, Zdenek Sekera,= Preben=20 "Peppe" Guldberg, Steve Kirkendall, Shaul Karl and all others=20 who helped me with their comments.

    Feel free to send me (volontir=20 at yahoo dot com) your comments. suggestions, examples...

    III. Substitute Command
      =20

    3.1 Search & Replace

    So, what can you do with regular expressions? The most common task= is=20 to make replacements in a text following some certain rules. For th= is=20 tutorial you need to know VIM search and replace command (S&R) = :substitute.=20 Here is an excerpt from VIM help:

    =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20
    :range s[ubstitute]/pattern/string/cgiI=20
    For each line in the range replac= e a match=20 of the pattern with the string wher= e:
    =20
    c
    		 Confirm each substitution
    =20
    g
    		 Replace all occurrences in the line (without g -=20 only first).
    =20
    i
    		 Ignore case for the pattern.
    =20
    I
    		Don't ignore case for the pattern.

    Part of the command word enclosed in the "[" & "]"=20 can be omitted.

    3.2 Range of Operation, Line Addressing and M= arks

    Before I begin with a pattern description let's talk about line ad= dresses=20 in Vim. Some Vim commands can accept a line range in front of them.= By=20 specifying the line range you restrict the command execution to thi= s particular=20 part of text only. Line range consists of one or more line specifie= rs,=20 separated with a comma or semicolon. You can also mark your current= position=20 in the text typing ml , where "l"=20 can be any letter, and use it later defining the line address.

    =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20
    =20
    Specifier
    		 =20
    Description
    =20
    number
    		 an absolute line number
    =20
    .
    		the current line
    =20
    $
    		 the last line in the file
    =20
    %
    		 the whole file. The same as 1,$
    =20
    't
    		position of mark "t"
    =20
    /pattern[/]
    		the next line where text "pattern" matches.
    =20
    ?pattern[?]
    		the previous line where text "pattern" matches
    =20
    \/
    		the next line where the previously used search pattern matche= s
    =20
    \?
    		 the previous line where the previously used search pattern m= atches=20
    =20
    \&
    		 the next line where the previously used substitute pattern m= atches=20

    If no line range is specified the command will operate on the cur= rent=20 line only.

    Here are a few examples:

    10,20

    - from 10 to 20 line.

    Each may be followed (several times) by "+" or "-"=20 and an optional number. This number is added or subtracted from the= preceding=20 line number. If the number is omitted, 1 is used.

    /Section 1/+,/Section 2/-

    - all lines between Section 1 and <= span class=3D"pattern">Section=20 2, non-inclusively, i.e. the lines containing Section=20 1 and Section 2 will not be a= ffected.=20

    The /pattern/ and ?pattern? may=20 be followed by another address separated by a semicolon. A semicolo= n between=20 two search patterns tells Vim to find the location of the first pat= tern,=20 then start searching from that location for the second pattern.

    /Section 1/;/Subsection/-,/Subsection/+

    - first find Section 1, then the fi= rst line=20 with Subsection, step one line down = (beginning=20 of the range) and find the next line with S= ubsection,=20 step one line up (end of the range).

    The next example shows how you can reuse you search pattern:

    :/Section/+ y

    - this will search for the Section = line=20 and yank (copy) one line after into the memory.

    :// normal p

    - and that will search for the next Sectio= n=20 line and put (paste) the saved text on the next line.
      =20
    =20

    Tip 1: frequently you need to do S&R in a text which = contains=20 UNIX file paths - text strings with slashes ("/") inside.=20 Because S&R command uses slashes for pattern/replacement sepa= ration=20 you have to escape every slash in your pattern, i.e. use "\/"=20 for every "/" in your pattern:

    s/\/dir1\/dir2\/dir3\/file/dir4\/dir5\/file2/g

    To avoid this so-called "backslashitis" you can use different= =20 separators in S&R (I prefer ":")

    s:/dir1/dir2/dir3/file:/dir4/dir5/file2:g

    Tip 2: You may find these mappings useful (put them in yo= ur=20 .vimrc file)

    noremap ;; :%s:::g<Left><Left><Left>
    noremap ;' :%s:::cg<Left><Left><Left><Left&g= t;    =20

    These mappings save you some keystrokes and put you where you st= art=20 typing your search pattern. After typing it you move to the repla= cement=20 part , type it and hit return. The second version adds confirmati= on=20 flag.
    =20

    IV. Pattern Description
      =20

    4.1 Anchors

    Suppose you want to replace all occurrences of vi=20 with VIM. This can be easily done wi= th

    s/vi/VIM/g

    If you've tried this example then you, no doubt, noticed that VIM=20 replaced all occurrences of vi even = if it's=20 a part of the word (e.g. navigator). I= f we=20 want to be more specific and replace only whole words vi=20 then we need to correct our pattern. We may rewrite it by putting s= paces=20 around vi:

    s: vi : VIM :g

    But it will still miss vi followed = by the=20 punctuation or at the end of the line/file. The right way is to put= special=20 word boundary symbols "\<" and "\>"= =20 around vi.

    s:\<vi\>:VIM:g

    The beginning and the end of the line have their own special ancho= rs=20 - "^" and "$", respectively.=20 So, for all vi only at the start of = the line:

    s:^vi\>:VIM:

    To match the lines where vi is the = only=20 word:

    s:^vi$:VIM:

    Now suppose you want to replace not only all vi=20 but also Vi and VI.=20 There are several ways to do this:

    • probably the simplest way is to put "i" - ignore case in=20 a pattern %s:vi:VIM:gi=20
    • define a class of characters. This is a sequence of charac= ters enclosed=20 by square brackets "[" and "]". It matches any character=20 from this set. So :%s:[Vv]i:VIM: will match vi=20 and Vi. More on character ranges i= n the=20 following section.=20
      =20

    4.2 "Escaped" characters or metacharac= ters

    So far our pattern strings were constructed from normal or l= iteral=20 text characters. The power of regexps is in the use of <= b>metacharacters.=20 These are types of characters which have special meaning inside= the=20 search pattern. With a few exceptions these metacharacters are dist= inguished=20 by a "magic" backslash in front of them. The table below lists=20 some common VIM metacharacters.

    =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20
    =20
    #
    		 =20
    Matching
    		 =20
    #
    		 =20
    Matching
    =20
    .
    		 any character except new line    
    =20
    \s
    		 whitespace character =20
    \S
    		 non-whitespace character
    =20
    \d
    		 digit =20
    \D
    		 non-digit
    =20
    \x
    		 hex digit =20
    \X
    		 non-hex digit
    =20
    \o
    		 octal digit =20
    \O
    		 non-octal digit
    =20
    \h
    		 head of word character (a,b,c...z,A,B,C...Z and= _) =20
    \H
    		 non-head of word character
    =20
    \p
    		 printable character =20
    \P
    		 like \p, but excluding digits
    =20
    \w
    		 word character =20
    \W
    		 non-word character
    =20
    \a
    		 alphabetic character =20
    \A
    		 non-alphabetic character
    =20
    \l
    		 lowercase character =20
    \L
    		 non-lowercase character
    =20
    \u
    		 uppercase character =20
    \U
    		 non-uppercase character

    So, to match a date like 09/01/2000 you can use (assuming you don'= t use=20 "/" as a separator in the S&R)

    \d\d/\d\d/\d\d\d\d

    To match 6 letter word starting with a capital letter

    \u\w\w\w\w\w

    Obviously, it is not very convenient to write \w for= any=20 character in the pattern - what if you don't know how many letters = in=20 your word? This can be helped by introducing so-called quantifie= rs.=20

      =20

    4.3 Quantifiers, Greedy and Non-Greedy=

    Using quantifiers you can set how many times certain part of you p= attern=20 should repeat by putting the following after your pattern:

    =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20
    =20
    Quantifier
    		 =20
    Description
    =20
    *
    		 matches 0 or more of the preceding characters, ranges or meta= characters=20 .* matches everything including empty line
    =20
    \+
    		 matches 1 or more of the preceding characters...
    =20
    \=3D
    		matches 0 or 1 more of the preceding characters...
    =20
    \{n,m}
    		 matches from n to m of the preceding characters...
    =20
    \{n}
    		 matches exactly n times of the preceding characters...
    =20
    \{,m}
    		 matches at most m (from 0 to m) of the preceding characters..= .
    =20
    \{n,}
    		 matches at least n of of the preceding characters...
    =20
    where n and m are positive = integers=20 (>0)

    Now it's much easier to define a pattern that matches a word of any=20 length \u\w\+.

    These quantifiers are greedy - that is your pattern will tr= y to=20 match as much text as possible. Sometimes it present= s a=20 problem. Let's consider a typical example - define a pattern to mat= ch=20 delimited text, i.e. text enclosed in quotes, brackets, etc. Since = we=20 don't know what kind of text is inside the quotes we'll use

    /".*"/

    But this pattern will match everything between the first "= =20 and the last " in the following line:

    this file is normally "$VIM/.gvimrc".= You=20 can check this with ":version".

    This problem can be resolved by using non-greedy quantifiers:

    =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20
    =20
    Quantifier
    		 =20
    Description
    =20
    \{-}
    		 matches 0 or more of the preceding atom, as few as possible =
    =20
    \{-n,m}
    		matches 1 or more of the preceding characters...
    =20
    \{-n,}
    		 matches at lease or more of the preceding characters...
    =20
    \{-,m}
    		matches 1 or more of the preceding characters...
    =20
    where n and m are positive = integers=20 (>0)

    Let's use \{-} in place of * in our patt= ern.=20 So, now ".\{-}" will match the first quoted text:

    this file is normally "$VIM/gvimrc".=20 You can check this with ":version".

    .\{-} pattern is not without surprises. Look what wil= l happen=20 to the following text after we apply:

    :s:.\{-}:_:g

    Before:

    n and m are decimal numbers between

    After:

    _n_ _a_n_d_ _m_ _a_r_e_ _d_e_c_i_m_a_l_ _n_u_m_b_e_r_s_ _b_= e_t_w_e_e_n_

    "As few as possible" applied here means zero character replacement= s.=20 However match does occur between characters! To explain this= behavior=20 I quote Bram himself:

    Matching zero characters is still a match. Thus it will repl= ace=20 zero characters with a "_". And then go on to the next position, wh= ere=20 it will match again.

    It's true that using "\{-}" is mostly useless. It works this= way=20 to be consistent with "*", which also matches zero characters. Ther= e are=20 more useless ones: "x\{-1,}" always matches one x. You could just u= se=20 "x". More useful is something like "x\{70}". The others are just co= nsistent=20 behavior: ..., "x\{-3,}", "x\{-2,}", "x\{-1,}.

    - Bram

    But what if we want to match only the second occurrence of quoted = text?=20 Or we want to replace only a part of the quoted text keeping the re= st=20 untouched? We will need grouping and backreferences. = But=20 before let's talk more about character ranges.
      =20

    4.4 Character ranges

    Typical character ranges:

    [012345] will match any of the numbers inside the bra= ckets.=20 The same range can be written as [0-5], where dash ind= icates=20 a range of characters in ASCII order. Likewise, we can define the r= ange=20 for all lowercase letters: [a-z], for all letters: [a-zA-Z],=20 letters and digits: [0-9a-zA-Z] etc. Depending on your= system=20 locale you can define range which will include characters like =E0,= =20 =D6, =DF and other non ASCII characters.

    Note that the range represents just one character in the se= arch=20 pattern, that is [0123] and 0123 are not = the=20 same. Likewise the order (with a few exceptions) is not important: = [3210]=20 and [0123] are the same character ranges, while = 0123=20 and 3210 are two different patterns. Watch what happen= s when=20 we apply

    s:[65]:Dig:g

    to the following text:

    Before:

    High 65 to 70. Southeast wind a= round=20 10

    After:

    High DigDig to 70. Southeast = wind=20 around 10

    and now:

    s:65:Dig:g

    Before:

    High 65 to 70. Southeast wind a= round=20 10

    After:

    High Dig to 70. Southeast win= d around=20 10

    Sometimes it's easier to define the characters you don't want to m= atch.=20 This is done by putting a negation sign "^" (caret)=20 as a first character of the range

    /[^A-Z]/

    - will match any character except capital letters. We can = now=20 rewrite our pattern for quoted text using

    /"[^"]\+"/

    Note: inside the [ ] all metacharacters behave like ordinary chara= cters.=20 If you want to include "-" (dash) in your range put it first

    /[-0-9]/

    - will match all digits and -. "^" will lose its special= =20 meaning if it's not the first character in the range.

    Now, let's have some real life example. Suppose you want to run a = grammar=20 check on your file and find all places where new sentence does not = start=20 with a capital letter. The pattern that will catch this:

    \.\s\+[a-z]

    - a period followed by one or more blanks and a lowercase word. We= know=20 how to find an error, now let's see how we can correct it. To do th= is=20 we need some ways to remember our matched pattern and recall it lat= er.=20 That is exactly what backreferences are for.

      =20

    4.5 Grouping and Backreferences

    You can group parts of the pattern expression enclosing them with = "\("=20 and "\)" and refer to them inside the replacement=20 pattern by their special number \1, \2 ... \9. Typical= example=20 is swapping first two words of the line:

    s:\(\w\+\)\(\s\+\)\(\w\+\):\3\2\1:=20

    where \1 holds the first word, \2 - any = number=20 of spaces or tabs in between and \3 - the second word.= How=20 to decide what number holds what pair of \(\) ? - coun= t opening=20 "\(" from the left.

    Replacement Part of :substitute

    Replacement part of the S&R has its own special characters whi= ch=20 we are going to use to fix grammar:

    =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20 =20
    =20
    #
    		 =20
    Meaning
    		 =20
    #
    		 =20
    Meaning
    =20
    &
    		 the whole matched pattern =20
    \L
    		 the following characters are made lowercase
    =20
    \0
    		 the whole matched pattern =20
    \U
    		 the following characters are made uppercase
    =20
    \1
    		 the matched pattern in the first pair of \(\) =20
    \E
    		 end of \U and \L
    =20
    \2
    		 the matched pattern in the second pair of \(\)<= /td> =20
    \e
    		 end of \U and \L
    =20
    ...
    		 ... =20
    \r
    		 split line in two at this point
    =20
    \9
    		 the matched pattern in the ninth pair of \(\) =20
    \l
    		next character made lowercase
    =20
    ~
    		 the previous substitute string =20
    \u
    		 next character made uppercase

    Now the full S&R to correct non-capital words at the beginning= of=20 the sentences looks like

    s:\([.!?]\)\s\+\([a-z]\):\1  \u\2:g

    We have corrected our grammar and as an extra job we replaced vari= able=20 number of spaces between punctuation and the first letter of the ne= xt=20 sentence with exactly two spaces.
      =20

    4.6 Alternations

    Using "\|" you can combine several expressions=20 into one which matches any of its components. The first one matched= will=20 be used.

    \(Date:\|Subject:\|From:\)\(\s.*\)

    will parse various mail headings and their contents into \1 and \2, r= espectively.=20 The thing to remember about VIM alternation that it is not greedy.= =20 It won't search for the longest possible match, it will use the first= that=20 matched. That means that the order of the items in the alternation is= important!
      =20
    =20

    Tip 3: Quick mapping to put \(\) in your pattern string

    cmap ;\ \(\)<Left><Left>
      =20

    4.7 Regexp Operator Precedence

    As in arithmetic expressions, regular expressions are executed in = a certain=20 order of precedence. Here the table of precedence, from highest to = lowest:

    =20 =20 =20 =20 =20
    =20
    Precedence
    		 =20
    Regexp
    		 =20
    Description
    =20
    1
    		 =20
    \( \)
    		 =20
    grouping
    =20
    2
    		 =20
    \=3D,\+,*,\{n} etc.
    		 =20
    quantifiers
    =20
    3
    		 =20
    abc\t\.\w
    		 =20
    sequence of characters/ metacharacters, n= ot containing=20 quantifiers or grouping operators
    =20
    4
    		 =20
    \|
    		 =20
    alternation
    =20

    V. Global Command
      =20

    5.1 Global search and execution

    I want to introduce another quite useful and powerful Vim command = which=20 we're going to use later

    =20 =20
    :range g[lobal][!]/pattern/= cmd
    Execute the Ex command cmd (defau= lt ":p")=20 on the lines within [range] where pattern=20 matches. If pattern is preceded with a ! -= only=20 where match does not occur.

    The global commands work by first scanning through the [range]=20 of of the lines and marking each line where a match occurs. In a se= cond=20 scan the [cmd] is executed for each marked line with its lin= e number=20 prepended. If a line is changed or deleted its mark disappears. The= default=20 for the [range] is the whole file.

    Note: Ex commands are all commands you are entering on the Vim com= mand=20 line like :s[ubstitute], :co[py] , :d[elete], :w[rite] etc.=20 Non-Ex commands (normal mode commands) can be also executed via

    :norm[al]non-ex command

    mechanism.

    5.2 Examples

    Some examples of :global usage:

    :g/^$/ d

    - delete all empty lines in a file

    :g/^$/,/./-j

    - reduce multiple blank lines to a single blank

    :10,20g/^/ mo 10

    - reverse the order of the lines starting from the line 10 up to t= he=20 line 20.

    Here is a modified example from Walter=20 Zintz vi tutorial:

    :'a,'b g/^Error/ . w >> errors.txt

    - in the text block marked by 'a and 'b = find=20 all the lines starting with Error an= d copy=20 (append) them to "errors.txt" file. Note: . (current=20 line address) in front of the w is very important, omi= tting=20 it will cause :write to write the whole file to "error= s.txt"=20 for every Error line found.

    You can give multiple commands after :global using "|= "=20 as a separator. If you want to use "|' in an argument, precede it= =20 with "\'. Another example from Zintz tutorial:

    :g/^Error:/ copy $ | s /Error/copy of the error/

    - will copy all Error line to the e= nd of=20 the file and then make a substitution in the copied line. Without g= iving=20 the line address :s will operate on the current line, = which=20 is the newly copied line.

    :g/^Error:/ s /Error/copy of the error/ | copy $

    - here the order is reversed: first modify the string then copy to= the=20 end.
       
    =20

    VI. Examples
      =20

    6.1 Tips and Techniques

    A collection of some useful S&R tips:

    (1) sent by Antonio Colombo:

    "a simple regexp I use quite often to clean up a text: it= =20 drops the blanks at the end of the line:"

    s:\s*$::

    or (to avoid acting on all lines):

    s:\s\+$::
      =20

    6.2 Creating outline

    For this example you need to know a bit of HTML. We want to make a= table=20 of contents out of h1 and h2 headings, wh= ich=20 I will call majors and minors. HTML heading h1 is a te= xt=20 enclosed by <h1> tags as in <h1>Head= ing</h1>.

    (1) First let's make named anchors in all headings, i.e. put <h1><a=20 name=3D"anchor">Heading</a></h1> around=20 all headings. The "anchor" is a unique identifier=20 of this particular place in HTML document. The following S&R do= es=20 exactly this:

    :s:\(<h[12]>\)\(.*\s\+\([-a-zA-Z]\+\)\)\s*\(</h[12= ]>\):\1<a=20 name=3D"\3">\2</a>\4:

    Explanation: the first pair of \(\) saves the = opening=20 tag (h1 or h2) to the \1, th= e second=20 pair saves all heading text before the closing tag, the third pair = saves=20 the last word in the heading which we will later use for "anchor"= =20 and the last pair saves the closing tag. The replacement is quite o= bvious=20 - we just reconstruct a new "named" heading using \1-\4=20 and link tag <a>.

    (2) Now let's copy all headings to one place:

    :%g/<h[12]>/ t$

    This command searches our file for the lines starting with &= lt;h1>=20 or <h2> and copies them to the end of the file. = Now=20 we have a bunch of lines like:

    <h1><a name=3D"anchor1">Heading1></a>&l= t;/h1>
    <h2><a name=3D"anchor2">Heading2></a></h2&g= t;
    <h2><a name=3D"anchor3">Heading3></a></h2&g= t;
    ..........................
    <h1><a name=3D"anchorN">HeadingN></a></h1&g= t;    =20

    First, we want to convert all name=3D" to href= =3D"#=20 in order to link table entries to their respective places in the te= xt:

    s:name=3D":href=3D"#:

    Second, we want our h1 entries look different from h2.=20 Let's define CSS classes "majorhead" and "minorhead"=20 and do the following:

    g/<h1>/ s:<a:& class=3D"majorhead":
    g/<h2>/ s:<a:& class=3D"minorhead":

    Now our entries look like:

    <h1><a class=3D"majorhead" name=3D"anchor1">Head= ing1></a></h1>
    <h2><a class=3D"minorhead" name=3D"anchor2">Heading2>= ;</a></h2>

    We no longer need h1 and h2 tags:

    s:<h[21]>::

    and replace closing tags with breaklines <br>

    s:/h[21]:br:

    <a class=3D"majorhead" name=3D"anchor1">Heading1>&l= t;/a><br>
    <a class=3D"minorhead" name=3D"anchor2">Heading2></a>= ;<br>
      =20

    6.3 Working with Tables

    Quite often you have to work with a text organized in tables/colum= ns.=20 Consider, for example, the following text

    =20 =20 =20
    =20
    Asia
    		 =20
    America
    		 =20
    Africa
    		 =20
    Europe
    =20
    Africa
    		 =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Africa
    =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Asia
    		 =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Europe

    Suppose we want to change all "Europe" cells in the third column= =20 to "Asia":

    :%s:\(\(\w\+\s\+\)\{2}\)Europe:\1Asia:

    =20 =20 =20
    =20
    Asia
    		 =20
    America
    		 =20
    Africa
    		 =20
    Europe
    =20
    Africa
    		 =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Asia
    		 =20
    Africa
    =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Asia
    		 =20
    Asia
    		 =20
    Europe

     

    To swap the first and the last columns:

    :%s:\(\w\+\)\(.*\s\+\)\(\w\+\)$:\3\2\1:

    =20 =20 =20
    =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    America
    		 =20
    Africa
    		 =20
    Asia
    =20
    Africa
    		 =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Africa
    =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Asia
    		 =20
    Europe
    		 =20
    Europe

     

    To be continued...
       
    =20

    VII. Other Regexp Flavors
      =20

    Here I would like to compare Vim's regexp implementation with othe= rs,=20 in particular, Perl's. You can't talk about regular expressions wit= hout=20 mentioning Perl.

    (with a help from Steve = Kirkendall)=20 The main differences between Perl and Vim are:

    • Perl doesn't require backslashes before most of its operators.= Personally,=20 I think it makes regexps more readable - the less backlashes are = there=20 the better.
    • Perl allows you to convert any quantifier into a non-greedy ver= sion=20 by adding an extra ? after it. So *? is a non-greedy *.
    • Perl supports a lots of weird options that can be appended to t= he=20 regexp, or even embedded in it.
    • You can also embed variable names in a Perl regular expression= . Perl=20 replaces the name with its value; this is called "variable interp= olation".
    =20

    VIII. Links
      =20

    Read VIM documentation about pattern and searching. To get this ty= pe=20 ":help pattern" in VIM normal mode.

    There are currently two books on the market that deal with VIM reg= ular=20 expressions:

    • "Learning th= e=20 vi Editor" by Linda Lamb and Arnold Robbins.
    • "vi Improved - VIM" b= y Steve Oualline

    Definitive reference on regular expressions is Jeffrey Friedl's "Master= ing=20 Regular Expressions" published by O'Reilly & Associates= ,=20 but it mostly deals with Perl regular expressions. O'Reilly has one= of=20 the book chapters available online.
